                    }
                }

<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="Genome sequencing and analysis of the model grass Brachypodium distachyon"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Nature 2010 463:7282"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2010 Macmillan Publishers Limited. All rights reserved"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="Three subfamilies of grasses, the Ehrhartoideae, Panicoideae and Pooideae, provide the bulk of human nutrition and are poised to become major sources of renewable energy. Here we describe the genome sequence of the wild grass Brachypodium distachyon (Brachypodium), which is, to our knowledge, the first member of the Pooideae subfamily to be sequenced. Comparison of the Brachypodium, rice and sorghum genomes shows a precise history of genome evolution across a broad diversity of the grasses, and establishes a template for analysis of the large genomes of economically important pooid grasses such as wheat. The high-quality genome sequence, coupled with ease of cultivation and transformation, small size and rapid life cycle, will help Brachypodium reach its potential as an important model system for developing new energy and food crops. The genome of Brachypodium distachyon, a wild annual grass endemic to the Mediterranean and Middle East, has been sequenced. This is the first member of the economically important Pooideae subfamily, which includes wheat and barley, to be sequenced. Comparative genomics using the Brachypodium, rice and sorghum genomes provide insights into genome evolution of the grasses and will aid future analyses of large and complex genomes such as that of wheat. Availability of this genome sequence should help establish Brachypodium, which is easily cultivated and amenable to genetic manipulation, as a model for developing new energy and food crops. The genome of the wild grass Brachypodium distachyon (Brachypodium), a member of the Pooideae subfamily, is sequenced. The Pooideae are one of three subfamilies of grasses that provide the bulk of human nutrition and may become major sources of renewable energy. Availability of the genome sequence should help establish Brachypodium as a model for developing new energy and food crops."/>

        <meta property="og:title" content="Genome sequencing and analysis of the model grass Brachypodium distachyon"/>
        <meta property="og:description" content="The genome of the wild grass Brachypodium distachyon (Brachypodium), a member of the Pooideae subfamily, is sequenced. The Pooideae are one of three subfamilies of grasses that provide the bulk of human nutrition and may become major sources of renewable energy. Availability of the genome sequence should help establish Brachypodium as a model for developing new energy and food crops."/>

                                        <span>Sign up for alerts</span><svg role="img" aria-hidden="true" focusable="false" height="18" viewBox="0 0 18 18" width="18" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg"><path d="m4 10h2.5c.27614237 0 .5.2238576.5.5s-.22385763.5-.5.5h-3.08578644l-1.12132034 1.1213203c-.18753638.1875364-.29289322.4418903-.29289322.7071068v.1715729h14v-.1715729c0-.2652165-.1053568-.5195704-.2928932-.7071068l-1.7071068-1.7071067v-3.4142136c0-2.76142375-2.2385763-5-5-5-2.76142375 0-5 2.23857625-5 5zm3 4c0 1.1045695.8954305 2 2 2s2-.8954305 2-2zm-5 0c-.55228475 0-1-.4477153-1-1v-.1715729c0-.530433.21071368-1.0391408.58578644-1.4142135l1.41421356-1.4142136v-3c0-3.3137085 2.6862915-6 6-6s6 2.6862915 6 6v3l1.4142136 1.4142136c.3750727.3750727.5857864.8837805.5857864 1.4142135v.1715729c0 .5522847-.4477153 1-1 1h-4c0 1.6568542-1.3431458 3-3 3-1.65685425 0-3-1.3431458-3-3z" fill="#222"/></svg>

                    <h1 class="c-article-title" data-test="article-title" data-article-title="" itemprop="name headline">Genome sequencing and analysis of the model grass <i>Brachypodium distachyon</i></h1>
                    <ul class="c-author-list js-etal-collapsed" data-etal="25" data-etal-small="3" data-test="authors-list" data-component-authors-activator="authors-list"><li class="c-author-list__item"><a data-test="author-name" data-author-popup="group-1" href="#group-1">The International Brachypodium Initiative</a></li></ul>

                    

                    <p class="c-article-info-details" data-container-section="info">
                        
    <a data-test="journal-link" href="/nature"><i data-test="journal-title">Nature</i></a>

                        <b data-test="journal-volume"><span class="u-visually-hidden">volume</span> 463</b>, <span class="u-visually-hidden">pages</span><span itemprop="pageStart">763</span>–<span itemprop="pageEnd">768</span>(<span data-test="article-publication-year">2010</span>)<a href="#citeas" class="c-article-info-details__cite-as u-hide-print" data-track="click" data-track-action="cite this article" data-track-label="link">Cite this article</a>
                    </p>

            <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><p>Three subfamilies of grasses, the Ehrhartoideae, Panicoideae and Pooideae, provide the bulk of human nutrition and are poised to become major sources of renewable energy. Here we describe the genome sequence of the wild grass <i>Brachypodium distachyon</i> (<i>Brachypodium</i>), which is, to our knowledge, the first member of the Pooideae subfamily to be sequenced. Comparison of the <i>Brachypodium</i>, rice and sorghum genomes shows a precise history of genome evolution across a broad diversity of the grasses, and establishes a template for analysis of the large genomes of economically important pooid grasses such as wheat. The high-quality genome sequence, coupled with ease of cultivation and transformation, small size and rapid life cycle, will help <i>Brachypodium</i> reach its potential as an important model system for developing new energy and food crops.</p></div></div></section>
                <noscript>

                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Main"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Main</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>Grasses provide the bulk of human nutrition, and highly productive grasses are promising sources of sustainable energy<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Somerville, C. The billion-ton biofuels vision. Science 312, 1277 (2006)" href="/articles/nature08747#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d3871e3023">1</a></sup>. The grass family (Poaceae) comprises over 600 genera and more than 10,000 species that dominate many ecological and agricultural systems<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Kellogg, E. A. Evolutionary history of the grasses. Plant Physiol. 125, 1198–1205 (2001)" href="/articles/nature08747#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d3871e3027">2</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Gaut, B. S. Evolutionary dynamics of grass genomes. New Phytol. 154, 15–28 (2002)" href="/articles/nature08747#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d3871e3030">3</a></sup>. So far, genomic efforts have largely focused on two economically important grass subfamilies, the Ehrhartoideae (rice) and the Panicoideae (maize, sorghum, sugarcane and millets). The rice<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="International Rice Genome Sequencing Project. The map-based sequence of the rice genome. Nature 436, 793–800 (2005)" href="/articles/nature08747#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d3871e3034">4</a></sup> and sorghum<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Paterson, A. H. et al. The Sorghum bicolor genome and the diversification of grasses. Nature 457, 551–556 (2009)" href="/articles/nature08747#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d3871e3038">5</a></sup> genome sequences and a detailed physical map of maize<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Wei, F. et al. Physical and genetic structure of the maize genome reflects its complex evolutionary history. PLoS Genet. 3, e123 (2007)" href="/articles/nature08747#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d3871e3042">6</a></sup> showed extensive conservation of gene order<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Paterson, A. H. et al. The Sorghum bicolor genome and the diversification of grasses. Nature 457, 551–556 (2009)" href="/articles/nature08747#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d3871e3047">5</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Moore, G., Devos, K. M., Wang, Z. &amp; Gale, M. D. Cereal genome evolution. Grasses, line up and form a circle. Curr. Biol. 5, 737–739 (1995)" href="/articles/nature08747#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d3871e3050">7</a></sup> and both ancient and relatively recent polyploidization.</p><p>Most cool season cereal, forage and turf grasses belong to the Pooideae subfamily, which is also the largest grass subfamily. The genomes of many pooids are characterized by daunting size and complexity. For example, the bread wheat genome is approximately 17,000 megabases (Mb) and contains three independent genomes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Salamini, F., Ozkan, H., Brandolini, A., Schafer-Pregl, R. &amp; Martin, W. Genetics and geography of wild cereal domestication in the near east. Nature Rev. Genet. 3, 429–441 (2002)" href="/articles/nature08747#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d3871e3057">8</a></sup>. This has prohibited genome-scale comparisons spanning the three most economically important grass subfamilies.</p><p><i>Brachypodium</i>, a member of the Pooideae subfamily, is a wild annual grass endemic to the Mediterranean and Middle East<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Draper, J. et al. Brachypodium distachyon. A new model system for functional genomics in grasses. Plant Physiol. 127, 1539–1555 (2001)" href="/articles/nature08747#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d3871e3066">9</a></sup> that has promise as a model system. This has led to the development of highly efficient transformation<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Vain, P. et al. Agrobacterium-mediated transformation of the temperate grass Brachypodium distachyon (genotype Bd21) for T-DNA insertional mutagenesis. Plant Biotechnol. J. 6, 236–245 (2008)" href="/articles/nature08747#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d3871e3070">10</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Vogel, J. &amp; Hill, T. High-efficiency Agrobacterium-mediated transformation of Brachypodium distachyon inbred line Bd21–3. Plant Cell Rep. 27, 471–478 (2008)" href="/articles/nature08747#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d3871e3073">11</a></sup>, germplasm collections<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Vogel, J. P., Garvin, D. F., Leong, O. M. &amp; Hayden, D. M. Agrobacterium-mediated transformation and inbred line development in the model grass Brachypodium distachyon . Plant Cell Tissue Organ Cult. 84, 100179–100191 (2006)" href="/articles/nature08747#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d3871e3077">12</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Filiz, E. et al. Molecular, morphological and cytological analysis of diverse Brachypodium distachyon inbred lines. Genome 52, 876–890 (2009)" href="/articles/nature08747#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d3871e3080">13</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Vogel, J. P. et al. Development of SSR markers and analysis of diversity in Turkish populations of Brachypodium distachyon . BMC Plant Biol. 9, 88 (2009)" href="/articles/nature08747#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d3871e3083">14</a></sup>, genetic markers<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Vogel, J. P. et al. Development of SSR markers and analysis of diversity in Turkish populations of Brachypodium distachyon . BMC Plant Biol. 9, 88 (2009)" href="/articles/nature08747#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d3871e3087">14</a></sup>, a genetic linkage map<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Garvin, D. F. et al. An SSR-based genetic linkage map of the model grass Brachypodium distachyon . Genome 53, 1–13 (2009)" href="/articles/nature08747#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d3871e3091">15</a></sup>, bacterial artificial chromosome (BAC) libraries<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Huo, N. et al. Construction and characterization of two BAC libraries from Brachypodium distachyon, a new model for grass genomics. Genome 49, 1099–1108 (2006)" href="/articles/nature08747#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d3871e3096">16</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Huo, N. et al. The nuclear genome of Brachypodium distachyon: analysis of BAC end sequences. Funct. Integr. Genomics 8, 135–147 (2008)" href="/articles/nature08747#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d3871e3099">17</a></sup>, physical maps<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Gu, Y. Q. et al. A BAC-based physical map of Brachypodium distachyon and its comparative analysis with rice and wheat. BMC Genomics 10, 496 (2009)" href="/articles/nature08747#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d3871e3103">18</a></sup> (M.F., unpublished observations), mutant collections (<a href="http://brachypodium.pw.usda.gov">http://brachypodium.pw.usda.gov</a>, <a href="http://www.brachytag.org">http://www.brachytag.org</a>), microarrays and databases (<a href="http://www.brachybase.org">http://www.brachybase.org</a>, <a href="http://www.phytozome.net">http://www.phytozome.net</a>, <a href="http://www.modelcrop.org">http://www.modelcrop.org</a>, <a href="http://mips.helmholtz-muenchen.de/plant/index.jsp">http://mips.helmholtz-muenchen.de/plant/index.jsp</a>) that are facilitating the use of <i>Brachypodium</i> by the research community. The genome sequence described here will allow <i>Brachypodium</i> to act as a powerful functional genomics resource for the grasses. It is also an important advance in grass structural genomics, permitting, for the first time, whole-genome comparisons between members of the three most economically important grass subfamilies.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Genome sequence assembly and annotation"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Genome sequence assembly and annotation</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><p>The diploid inbred line Bd21 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Garvin, D. F. et al. Development of genetic and genomic research resources for Brachypodium distachyon, a new model system for grass crop research. Crop Sci. 48, S-69–S-84 (2008)" href="/articles/nature08747#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d3871e3163">19</a>) was sequenced using whole-genome shotgun sequencing (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature08747#MOESM267">Supplementary Table 1</a>). The ten largest scaffolds contained 99.6% of all sequenced nucleotides (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature08747#MOESM267">Supplementary Table 2</a>). Comparison of these ten scaffolds with a genetic map (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature08747#MOESM267">Supplementary Fig. 1</a>) detected two false joins and created a further seven joins to produce five pseudomolecules that spanned 272 Mb (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature08747#MOESM267">Supplementary Table 3</a>), within the range measured by flow cytometry<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Bennett, M. D. &amp; Leitch, I. J. Nuclear DNA amounts in angiosperms: progress, problems and prospects. Ann. Bot. (Lond.) 95, 45–90 (2005)" href="/articles/nature08747#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d3871e3180">20</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Vogel, J. P. et al. EST sequencing and phylogenetic analysis of the model grass Brachypodium distachyon . Theor. Appl. Genet. 113, 186–195 (2006)" href="/articles/nature08747#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d3871e3183">21</a></sup>. The assembly was confirmed by cytogenetic analysis (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature08747#MOESM267">Supplementary Fig. 2</a>) and alignment with two physical maps and sequenced BACs (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature08747#MOESM267">Supplementary Data</a>). More than 98% of expressed sequence tags (ESTs) mapped to the sequence assembly, consistent with a near-complete genome (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature08747#MOESM267">Supplementary Table 4</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature08747#MOESM267">Supplementary Fig. 3</a>). Compared to other grasses, the <i>Brachypodium</i> genome is very compact, with retrotransposons concentrated at the centromeres and syntenic breakpoints (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature08747#Fig1">Fig. 1</a>). DNA transposons and derivatives are broadly distributed and primarily associated with gene-rich regions.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="
                        Chromosomal distribution of the main 
                        Brachypodium
                         genome features.
                      "><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 1: 
                        <b>Chromosomal distribution of the main </b>
                        <b><i>Brachypodium</i></b>
                        <b> genome features.</b>
                      </b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nature08747/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature08747/MediaObjects/41586_2010_Article_BFnature08747_Fig1_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature08747/MediaObjects/41586_2010_Article_BFnature08747_Fig1_HTML.jpg" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="1194"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p>The abundance and distribution of the following genome elements are shown: complete LTR retroelements (cLTRs); solo-LTRs (sLTRs); potentially autonomous DNA transposons that are not miniature inverted-repeat transposable elements (MITEs) (DNA-TEs); MITEs; gene exons (CDS); gene introns and satellite tandem arrays (STA). Graphs are from 0 to 100 per cent base-pair (%bp) coverage of the respective window. The heat map tracks have different ranges and different maximum (max) pseudocolour levels: STA (0–55, scaled to max 10) %bp; cLTRs (0–36, scaled to max 20) %bp; sLTRs (0–4) %bp; DNA-TEs (0–20) %bp; MITEs (0–22) %bp; CDS (exons) (0–22.3) %bp. The triangles identify syntenic breakpoints.</p><p>
                        <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature08747#MOESM263">PowerPoint slide</a>
                      </p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nature08747/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>We analysed small RNA populations from inflorescence tissues with deep Illumina sequencing, and mapped them onto the genome sequence (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature08747#Fig2">Fig. 2a</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature08747#MOESM267">Supplementary Fig. 4</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature08747#MOESM267">Supplementary Table 5</a>). Small RNA reads were most dense in regions of high repeat density, similar to the distribution reported in <i>Arabidopsis</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Rajagopalan, R., Vaucheret, H., Trejo, J. &amp; Bartel, D. P. A diverse and evolutionarily fluid set of microRNAs in Arabidopsis thaliana. Genes Dev. 20, 3407–3425 (2006)" href="/articles/nature08747#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d3871e3255">22</a></sup>. We identified 413 and 198 21- and 24-nucleotide phased short interfering RNA (siRNA) loci, respectively. Using the same algorithm, the only phased loci identified in <i>Arabidopsis</i> were five of the eight <i>trans</i>-acting siRNA loci, and none was 24-nucelotide phased. The biological functions of these clusters of <i>Brachypodium</i> phased siRNAs, which account for a significant number of small RNAs that map outside repeat regions, are not known at present.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="
                        Transcript and gene identification and distribution among three grass subfamilies.
                      "><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 2: 
                        <b>Transcript and gene identification and distribution among three grass subfamilies.</b>
                      </b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nature08747/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature08747/MediaObjects/41586_2010_Article_BFnature08747_Fig2_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature08747/MediaObjects/41586_2010_Article_BFnature08747_Fig2_HTML.jpg" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="791"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p><b>a</b>, Genome-wide distribution of small RNA loci and transcripts in the <i>Brachypodium</i> genome. <i>Brachypodium</i> chromosomes (1–5) are shown at the top. Total small RNA reads (black lines) and total small RNA loci (red lines) are shown on the top panel. Histograms plot 21-nucleotide (nt) (blue) or 24-nucleotide (red) small RNA reads normalized for repeated matches to the genome. The phased loci histograms plot the position and phase-score of 21-nucleotide (blue) and 24-nucleotide (red) phased small RNA loci. Repeat-normalized RNA-seq read histograms plot the abundance of reads matching RNA transcripts (green), normalized for ambiguous matches to the genome. <b>b</b>, Transcript coverage over gene features. Perfect match 32-base oligonucleotide Illumina reads were mapped to the <i>Brachypodium</i> v1.0 annotation features using HashMatch (<a href="http://mocklerlab-tools.cgrb.oregonstate.edu/">http://mocklerlab-tools.cgrb.oregonstate.edu/</a>). Plots of Illumina coverage were calculated as the percentage of bases along the length of the sequence feature supported by Illumina reads for the indicated gene model features. The bottom and top of the box represent the 25th and 75th quartiles, respectively. The white line is the median and the red diamonds denote the mean. SJS, splice junction site. <b>c</b>, Venn diagram showing the distribution of shared gene families between representatives of Ehrhartoideae (rice RAP2), Panicoideae (sorghum v1.4) and Pooideae (<i>Brachypodium</i> v1.0, and <i>Triticum aestivum</i> and <i>Hordeum vulgare</i> TCs (transcript consensus)/EST sequences). Paralogous gene families were collapsed in these data sets.</p><p>
                        <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature08747#MOESM264">PowerPoint slide</a>
                      </p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nature08747/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>A total of 25,532 protein-coding gene loci was predicted in the v1.0 annotation (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature08747#MOESM267">Supplementary Information</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature08747#MOESM267">Supplementary Table 6</a>). This is in the same range as rice (RAP2, 28,236)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Tanaka, T. et al. The rice annotation project database (RAP-DB): 2008 update. Nucleic Acids Res. 36, D1028–D1033 (2008)" href="/articles/nature08747#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d3871e3342">23</a></sup> and sorghum (v1.4, 27,640)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Paterson, A. H. et al. The Sorghum bicolor genome and the diversification of grasses. Nature 457, 551–556 (2009)" href="/articles/nature08747#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d3871e3346">5</a></sup>, suggesting similar gene numbers across a broad diversity of grasses. Gene models were evaluated using <span class="stix">∼</span>10.2 gigabases (Gb) of Illumina RNA-seq data (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature08747#MOESM267">Supplementary Fig. 5</a>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Fox, S., Filichkin, S. &amp; Mockler, T. Applications of ultra-high-throughput sequencing. Methods Mol. Biol. 553, 79–108 (2009)" href="/articles/nature08747#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d3871e3354">24</a></sup>. Overall, 92.7% of predicted coding sequences (CDS) were supported by Illumina data (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature08747#Fig2">Fig. 2b</a>), demonstrating the high accuracy of the <i>Brachypodium</i> gene predictions. These gene models are available from several databases (such as <a href="http://www.brachybase.org">http://www.brachybase.org</a>, <a href="http://www.phytozome.net">http://www.phytozome.net</a>, <a href="http://www.modelcrop.org">http://www.modelcrop.org</a> and <a href="http://mips.org">http://mips.org</a>).</p><p>Between 77 and 84% of gene families (defined according to <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature08747#MOESM267">Supplementary Fig. 6</a>) are shared among the three grass subfamilies represented by <i>Brachypodium</i>, rice and sorghum, reflecting a relatively recent common origin (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature08747#Fig2">Fig. 2c</a>). Grass-specific genes include transmembrane receptor protein kinases, glycosyltransferases, peroxidases and P450 proteins (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature08747#MOESM267">Supplementary Table 7B</a>). The Pooideae-specific gene set contains only 265 gene families (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature08747#MOESM267">Supplementary Table 7C</a>) comprising 811 genes (1,400 including singletons). Genes enriched in grasses were significantly more likely to be contained in tandem arrays than random genes, demonstrating a prominent role for tandem gene expansion in the evolution of grass-specific genes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature08747#MOESM267">Supplementary Fig. 7</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature08747#MOESM267">Supplementary Table 8</a>).</p><p>To validate and improve the v1.0 gene models, we manually annotated 2,755 gene models from 97 diverse gene families (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature08747#MOESM267">Supplementary Tables 9–11</a>) relevant to bioenergy and food crop improvement. We annotated 866 genes involved in cell wall biosynthesis/modification and 948 transcription factors from 16 families<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Gray, J. et al. A recommendation for naming transcription factor proteins in the grasses. Plant Physiol. 149, 4–6 (2009)" href="/articles/nature08747#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d3871e3425">25</a></sup>. Only 13% of the gene models required modification and very few pseudogenes were identified, demonstrating the accuracy of the v1.0 annotation. Phylogenetic trees for 62 gene families were constructed using genes from rice, <i>Arabidopsis</i>, sorghum and poplar. In nearly all cases, <i>Brachypodium</i> genes had a similar distribution to rice and sorghum, demonstrating that <i>Brachypodium</i> is suitably generic for grass functional genomics research (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature08747#MOESM267">Supplementary Figs 8 and 9</a>). Analysis of the predicted secretome identified substantial differences in the distribution of cell wall metabolism genes between dicots and grasses (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature08747#MOESM267">Supplementary Tables 12, 13</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature08747#MOESM267">Supplementary Fig. 10</a>), consistent with their different cell walls<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Vogel, J. Unique aspects of the grass cell wall. Curr. Opin. Plant Biol. 11, 301–307 (2008)" href="/articles/nature08747#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d3871e3448">26</a></sup>. Signal peptide probability curves also suggested that start codons were accurately predicted (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature08747#MOESM267">Supplementary Fig. 11</a>).</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec3" data-title="Maintaining a small grass genome size"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec3-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec3">Maintaining a small grass genome size</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec3-content"><p>Exhaustive analysis of transposable elements (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature08747#MOESM267">Supplementary Information</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature08747#MOESM267">Supplementary Table 14</a>) showed retrotransposon sequences comprise 21.4% of the genome, compared to 26% in rice, 54% in sorghum, and more than 80% in wheat<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Bennetzen, J. L. &amp; Kellogg, E. A. Do plants have a one-way ticket to genomic obesity? Plant Cell 9, 1509–1514 (1997)" href="/articles/nature08747#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d3871e3469">27</a></sup>. Thirteen retroelement sets were younger than 20,000 years, showing a recent activation compared to rice<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Wicker, T. &amp; Keller, B. Genome-wide comparative analysis of copia retrotransposons in Triticeae, rice, and Arabidopsis reveals conserved ancient evolutionary lineages and distinct dynamics of individual copia families. Genome Res. 17, 1072–1081 (2007)" href="/articles/nature08747#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d3871e3473">28</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature08747#MOESM267">Supplementary Fig. 12</a>), and a further 53 retroelement sets were less than 0.1 million years (Myr) old. A minimum of 17.4 Mb has been lost by long terminal repeat (LTR)–LTR recombination, demonstrating that retroelement expansion is countered by removal through recombination. In contrast, retroelements persist for very long periods of time in the closely related Triticeae<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Wicker, T. &amp; Keller, B. Genome-wide comparative analysis of copia retrotransposons in Triticeae, rice, and Arabidopsis reveals conserved ancient evolutionary lineages and distinct dynamics of individual copia families. Genome Res. 17, 1072–1081 (2007)" href="/articles/nature08747#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d3871e3481">28</a></sup>.</p><p>DNA transposons comprise 4.77% of the <i>Brachypodium</i> genome, within the range found in other grass genomes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Paterson, A. H. et al. The Sorghum bicolor genome and the diversification of grasses. Nature 457, 551–556 (2009)" href="/articles/nature08747#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d3871e3491">5</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Wicker, T. et al. Analysis of intraspecies diversity in wheat and barley genomes identifies breakpoints of ancient haplotypes and provides insight into the structure of diploid and hexaploid triticeae gene pools. Plant Physiol. 149, 258–270 (2009)" href="/articles/nature08747#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d3871e3494">29</a></sup>. Transcriptome data and structural analysis suggest that many non-autonomous <i>Mariner DTT</i> and <i>Harbinger</i> elements recruit transposases from other families. Two <i>CACTA DTC</i> families (M and N) carried five non-element genes, and the <i>Harbinger U</i> family has amplified a NBS-LRR gene family (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature08747#MOESM267">Supplementary Figs 13 and 14</a>), adding it to the group of transposable elements implicated in gene mobility<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Jiang, N., Bao, Z., Zhang, X., Eddy, S. R. &amp; Wessler, S. R. Pack-MULE transposable elements mediate gene evolution in plants. Nature 431, 569–573 (2004)" href="/articles/nature08747#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d3871e3514">30</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Morgante, M. et al. Gene duplication and exon shuffling by helitron-like transposons generate intraspecies diversity in maize. Nature Genet. 37, 997–1002 (2005)" href="/articles/nature08747#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d3871e3517">31</a></sup>. Centromeric regions were characterized by low gene density, characteristic repeats and retroelement clusters (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature08747#MOESM267">Supplementary Fig. 15</a>). Other repeat classes are described in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature08747#MOESM267">Supplementary Table 15</a>. Conserved non-coding sequences are described in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature08747#MOESM267">Supplementary Fig. 16</a>.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec4" data-title="Whole-genome comparison of three diverse grass genomes"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec4-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec4">Whole-genome comparison of three diverse grass genomes</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec4-content"><p>The evolutionary relationships between <i>Brachypodium</i>, sorghum, rice and wheat were assessed by measuring the mean synonymous substitution rates (<i>K</i><sub>s</sub>) of orthologous gene pairs (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature08747#MOESM267">Supplementary Information</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature08747#MOESM267">Supplementary Fig. 17</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature08747#MOESM267">Supplementary Table 16</a>), from which divergence times of <i>Brachypodium</i> from wheat 32–39 Myr ago, rice 40–53 Myr ago, and sorghum 45–60 Myr ago (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature08747#Fig3">Fig. 3a</a>) were estimated. The <i>K</i><sub>s</sub> of orthologous gene pairs in the intragenomic <i>Brachypodium</i> duplications (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature08747#Fig3">Fig. 3b</a>) suggests duplication 56–72 Myr ago, before the diversification of the grasses. This is consistent with previous evolutionary histories inferred from a small number of genes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Gaut, B. S. Evolutionary dynamics of grass genomes. New Phytol. 154, 15–28 (2002)" href="/articles/nature08747#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d3871e3572">3</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Grass Phylogeny Working Group. Phylogeny and subfamilial classification of the grasses (Poaceae). Ann. Mo. Bot. Gard. 88, 373–457 (2001)" href="/articles/nature08747#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d3871e3575">32</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Bossolini, E., Wicker, T., Knobel, P. A. &amp; Keller, B. Comparison of orthologous loci from small grass genomes Brachypodium and rice: implications for wheat genomics and grass genome annotation. Plant J. 49, 704–717 (2007)" href="/articles/nature08747#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d3871e3578">33</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Charles, M. et al. Sixty million years in evolution of soft grain trait in grasses: emergence of the softness locus in the common ancestor of Pooideae and Ehrhartoideae, after their divergence from Panicoideae . Mol. Biol. Evol. 26, 1651–1661 (2009)" href="/articles/nature08747#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d3871e3581">34</a></sup>.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="
                        Brachypodium
                         genome evolution and synteny between grass subfamilies.
                      "><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 3: 
                        <b><i>Brachypodium</i></b>
                        <b> genome evolution and synteny between grass subfamilies.</b>
                      </b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nature08747/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature08747/MediaObjects/41586_2010_Article_BFnature08747_Fig3_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature08747/MediaObjects/41586_2010_Article_BFnature08747_Fig3_HTML.jpg" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="600" height="1230"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p><b>a</b>, The distribution maxima of mean synonymous substitution rates (<i>K</i><sub>s</sub>) of <i>Brachypodium</i>, rice, sorghum and wheat orthologous gene pairs (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature08747#MOESM267">Supplementary Table 16</a>) were used to define the divergence times of these species and the age of interchromosomal duplications in <i>Brachypodium</i>. WGD, whole-genome duplication. The numbers refer to the predicted divergence times measured as Myr ago by the NG or ML methods. <b>b</b>, Diagram showing the six major interchromosomal <i>Brachypodium</i> duplications, defined by 723 paralogous relationships, as coloured bands linking the five chromosomes. <b>c</b>, Identification of chromosome relationships between the <i>Brachypodium</i>, rice and sorghum genomes. Orthologous relationships between the 25,532 protein-coding <i>Brachypodium</i> genes, 7,216 sorghum orthologues (12 syntenic blocks), and 8,533 rice orthologues (12 syntenic blocks) were defined. Sets of collinear orthologous relationships are represented by a coloured band according to each <i>Brachypodium</i> chromosome (blue, chromosome (chr.) 1; yellow, chr. 2; violet, chr. 3; red, chr. 4; green, chr. 5). The white region in each <i>Brachypodium</i> chromosome represents the centromeric region. <b>d</b>, Orthologous gene relationships between <i>Brachypodium</i> and barley and <i>Ae. tauschii</i> were identified using genetically mapped ESTs. 2,516 orthologous relationships defined 12 syntenic blocks. These are shown as coloured bands. <b>e</b>, Orthologous gene relationships between <i>Brachypodium</i> and hexaploid bread wheat defined by 5,003 ESTs mapped to wheat deletion bins. Each set of orthologous relationships is represented by a band that is evenly spread across each deletion interval on the wheat chromosomes.</p><p>
                        <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature08747#MOESM265">PowerPoint slide</a>
                      </p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nature08747/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Paralogous relationships among <i>Brachypodium</i> chromosomes showed six major chromosomal duplications covering 92.1% of the genome (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature08747#Fig3">Fig. 3b</a>), representing ancestral whole-genome duplication<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Paterson, A. H., Bowers, J. E. &amp; Chapman, B. A. Ancient polyploidization predating divergence of the cereals, and its consequences for comparative genomics. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 101, 9903–9908 (2004)" href="/articles/nature08747#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d3871e3680">35</a></sup>. Using the rice and sorghum genome sequences, genetic maps of barley<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Stein, N. et al. A 1,000-loci transcript map of the barley genome: new anchoring points for integrative grass genomics. Theor. Appl. Genet. 114, 823–839 (2007)" href="/articles/nature08747#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d3871e3684">36</a></sup> and <i>Aegilops tauschii</i> (the D genome donor of hexaploid wheat)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Luo, M. C. et al. Genome comparisons reveal a dominant mechanism of chromosome number reduction in grasses and accelerated genome evolution in Triticeae. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 106, 15780–15785 (2009)" href="/articles/nature08747#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d3871e3692">37</a></sup>, and bin-mapped wheat ESTs<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Qi, L. L. et al. A chromosome bin map of 16,000 expressed sequence tag loci and distribution of genes among the three genomes of polyploid wheat. Genetics 168, 701–712 (2004)" href="/articles/nature08747#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d3871e3696">38</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Salse, J. et al. Identification and characterization of shared duplications between rice and wheat provide new insight into grass genome evolution. Plant Cell 20, 11–24 (2008)" href="/articles/nature08747#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d3871e3699">39</a></sup>, 21,045 orthologous relationships between <i>Brachypodium</i>, rice, sorghum and Triticeae were identified (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature08747#MOESM267">Supplementary Information</a>). These identified 59 blocks of collinear genes covering 99.2% of the <i>Brachypodium</i> genome (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature08747#Fig3">Fig. 3c–e</a>). The orthologous relationships are consistent with an evolutionary model that shaped five <i>Brachypodium</i> chromosomes from a five-chromosome ancestral genome by a 12-chromosome intermediate involving seven major chromosome fusions<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Salse, J. et al. Identification and characterization of shared duplications between rice and wheat provide new insight into grass genome evolution. Plant Cell 20, 11–24 (2008)" href="/articles/nature08747#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d3871e3719">39</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature08747#MOESM267">Supplementary Fig. 18</a>). These collinear blocks of orthologous genes provide a robust and precise sequence framework for understanding grass genome evolution and aiding the assembly of sequences from other pooid grasses. We identified 14 major syntenic disruptions between <i>Brachypodium</i> and rice/sorghum that can be explained by nested insertions of entire chromosomes into centromeric regions (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature08747#Fig4">Fig. 4a, b</a>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Kellogg, E. A. Evolutionary history of the grasses. Plant Physiol. 125, 1198–1205 (2001)" href="/articles/nature08747#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d3871e3732">2</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Luo, M. C. et al. Genome comparisons reveal a dominant mechanism of chromosome number reduction in grasses and accelerated genome evolution in Triticeae. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 106, 15780–15785 (2009)" href="/articles/nature08747#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d3871e3735">37</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Srinivasachary, M. M., Gale, M. D. &amp; Devos, K. M. Comparative analyses reveal high levels of conserved colinearity between the finger millet and rice genomes. Theor. Appl. Genet. 115, 489–499 (2007)" href="/articles/nature08747#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d3871e3738">40</a></sup>. Similar nested insertions in sorghum<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Luo, M. C. et al. Genome comparisons reveal a dominant mechanism of chromosome number reduction in grasses and accelerated genome evolution in Triticeae. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 106, 15780–15785 (2009)" href="/articles/nature08747#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d3871e3743">37</a></sup> and barley (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature08747#Fig4">Fig. 4c, d</a>) were also identified. Centromeric repeats and peaks in retroelements at the junctions of chromosome insertions are footprints of these insertion events (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature08747#MOESM267">Supplementary Fig. 15C</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature08747#Fig1">Fig. 1</a>), as is higher gene density at the former distal regions of the inserted chromosomes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature08747#Fig1">Fig. 1</a>). Notably, the reduction in chromosome number in <i>Brachypodium</i> and wheat occurred independently because none of the chromosome fusions are shared by <i>Brachypodium</i> and the Triticeae<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Luo, M. C. et al. Genome comparisons reveal a dominant mechanism of chromosome number reduction in grasses and accelerated genome evolution in Triticeae. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 106, 15780–15785 (2009)" href="/articles/nature08747#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d3871e3766">37</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature08747#MOESM267">Supplementary Fig. 18</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="
                        A recurring pattern of nested chromosome fusions in grasses.
                      "><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 4: 
                        <b>A recurring pattern of nested chromosome fusions in grasses.</b>
                      </b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nature08747/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature08747/MediaObjects/41586_2010_Article_BFnature08747_Fig4_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature08747/MediaObjects/41586_2010_Article_BFnature08747_Fig4_HTML.jpg" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" width="685" height="684"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p><b>a</b>, The five <i>Brachypodium</i> chromosomes are coloured according to homology with rice chromosomes (Os1–Os12). Chromosomes descended from an ancestral chromosome (A4–A11) through whole-genome duplication are shown in shades of the same colour. Gene density is indicated as a red line above the chromosome maps. Major discontinuities in gene density identify syntenic breakpoints, which are marked by a diamond. White diamonds identify fusion points containing remnant centromeric repeats. <b>b</b>, A pattern of nested insertions of whole chromosomes into centromeric regions explains the observed syntenic break points. Bd5 has not undergone chromosome fusion. <b>c</b>, Examples of nested chromosome insertions in sorghum (Sb) chromosomes 1 and 2. <b>d</b>, Examples of nested chromosome insertions in barley (H chromosomes) inferred from genetic maps. Nested insertions were not identified in other chromosomes, possibly owing to the low resolution of genetic maps.</p><p>
                        <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature08747#MOESM266">PowerPoint slide</a>
                      </p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nature08747/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Comparisons of evolutionary rates between <i>Brachypodium</i>, sorghum, rice and <i>Ae. tauschii</i> demonstrated a substantially higher rate of genome change in <i>Ae. tauschii</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature08747#MOESM267">Supplementary Table 17</a>). This may be due to retroelement activity that increases syntenic disruptions, as proposed for chromosome 5S later<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Vicient, C. M., Kalendar, R. &amp; Schulman, A. H. Variability, recombination, and mosaic evolution of the barley BARE-1 retrotransposon. J. Mol. Evol. 61, 275–291 (2005)" href="/articles/nature08747#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d3871e3832">41</a></sup>. Among seven relatively large gene families, four were highly syntenic and two (NBS-LRR and F-box) were almost never found in syntenic order when compared to rice and sorghum (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature08747#MOESM267">Supplementary Table 18</a>), consistent with the rapid diversification of the NBS-LRR and F-box gene families<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Meyers, B. C., Kozik, A., Griego, A., Kuang, H. &amp; Michelmore, R. W. Genome-wide analysis of NBS-LRR-encoding genes in Arabidopsis . Plant Cell 15, 809–834 (2003)" href="/articles/nature08747#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d3871e3840">42</a></sup>.</p><p>The short arm of chromosome 5 (Bd5S) has a gene density roughly half of the rest of the genome, high LTR retrotransposon density, the youngest intact <i>Gypsy</i> elements and the lowest solo LTR density. Thus, unlike the rest of the <i>Brachypodium</i> genome, Bd5S is gaining retrotransposons by replication and losing fewer by recombination. Syntenic regions of rice (Os4S) and sorghum (Sb6S) demonstrate maintenance of this high repeat content for <span class="stix">∼</span>50–70 Myr (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature08747#MOESM267">Supplementary Fig. 19</a>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Ma, J. &amp; Bennetzen, J. L. Rapid recent growth and divergence of rice nuclear genomes. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 101, 12404–12410 (2004)" href="/articles/nature08747#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d3871e3856">43</a></sup>. Bd5S, Os4S and Sb6S also have the lowest proportion of collinear genes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature08747#Fig4">Fig. 4a</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature08747#MOESM267">Supplementary Fig. 19</a>). We propose that the chromosome ancestral to Bd5S reached a tipping point in which high retrotransposon density had deleterious effects on genes.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec5" data-title="Discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec5-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec5">Discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec5-content"><p>As the first genome sequence of a pooid grass, the <i>Brachypodium</i> genome aids genome analysis and gene identification in the large and complex genomes of wheat and barley, two other pooid grasses that are among the world’s most important crops. The very high quality of the <i>Brachypodium</i> genome sequence, in combination with those from two other grass subfamilies, enabled reconstruction of chromosome evolution across a broad diversity of grasses. This analysis contributes to our understanding of grass diversification by explaining how the varying chromosome numbers found in the major grass subfamilies derive from an ancestral set of five chromosomes by nested insertions of whole chromosomes into centromeres. The relatively small genome of <i>Brachypodium</i> contains many active retroelement families, but recombination between these keeps genome expansion in check. The short arm of chromosome 5 deviates from the rest of the genome by exhibiting a trend towards genome expansion through increased retroelement numbers and disruption of gene order more typical of the larger genomes of closely related grasses.</p><p>Grass crop improvement for sustainable fuel<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="U.S. Department of Energy Office of Science. Breaking the Biological Barriers to Cellulosic Ethanol: A Joint Research Agenda 〈 
                    http://genomicscience.energy.gov/biofuels/b2bworkshop.shtml
                    
                  〉 (2006)" href="/articles/nature08747#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d3871e3887">44</a></sup> and food<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Food and Agriculture Organization of the United Nations. World Agriculture: Towards 2030/2050 Interim Report 〈 
                    http://www.fao.org/ES/esd/AT2050web.pdf
                    
                  〉 (2006)" href="/articles/nature08747#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d3871e3891">45</a></sup> production requires a substantial increase in research in species such as <i>Miscanthus</i>, switchgrass, wheat and cool season forage grasses. These considerations have led to the rapid adoption of <i>Brachypodium</i> as an experimental system for grass research. The similarities in gene content and gene family structure between <i>Brachypodium</i>, rice and sorghum support the value of <i>Brachypodium</i> as a functional genomics model for all grasses. The <i>Brachypodium</i> genome sequence analysis reported here is therefore an important advance towards securing sustainable supplies of food, feed and fuel from new generations of grass crops.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec6" data-title="Methods Summary"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec6-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec6">Methods Summary</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec6-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7">Genome sequencing and assembly</h3><p>Sanger sequencing was used to generate paired-end reads from 3 kb, 8 kb, fosmid (35 kb) and BAC (100 kb) clones to generate 9.4× coverage (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature08747#MOESM267">Supplementary Table 1</a>). The final assembly of 83 scaffolds covers 271.9 Mb (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature08747#MOESM267">Supplementary Table 3</a>). Sequence scaffolds were aligned to a genetic map to create pseudomolecules covering each chromosome (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature08747#MOESM267">Supplementary Figs 1 and 2</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec8">Protein-coding gene annotation</h3><p>Gene models were derived from weighted consensus prediction from several <i>ab initio</i> gene finders, optimal spliced alignments of ESTs and transcript assemblies, and protein homology. Illumina transcriptome sequence was aligned to predicted genome features to validate exons, splice sites and alternatively spliced transcripts.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec9">Repeats analysis</h3><p>The MIPS ANGELA pipeline was used to integrate analyses from expert groups. LTR-STRUCT and LTR-HARVEST<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="McCarthy, E. M. &amp; McDonald, J. F. LTR_STRUC: a novel search and identification program for LTR retrotransposons. Bioinformatics 19, 362–367 (2003)" href="/articles/nature08747#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d3871e3952">46</a></sup> were used for <i>de novo</i> retroelement searches.</p></div></div></section>

Gale, who identified the importance of conserved gene order in grass genomes.             <aside><div class="c-article-editorial-summary__container" data-component-show-more="true" id="editorial-summary"><section><h1 class="c-article-editorial-summary__title u-h3">Editorial Summary</h1><h2 class="c-article-editorial-summary__article-title">Genome of a model grass</h2><div class="c-article-editorial-summary__content c-article-editorial-summary__content--less"><p>The genome of <i>Brachypodium distachyon</i>, a wild annual grass endemic to the Mediterranean and Middle East, has been sequenced. This is the first member of the economically important Pooideae subfamily, which includes wheat and barley, to be sequenced. Comparative genomics using the <i>Brachypodium</i>, rice and sorghum genomes provide insights into genome evolution of the grasses and will aid future analyses of large and complex genomes such as that of wheat. Availability of this genome sequence should help establish <i>Brachypodium</i>, which is easily cultivated and amenable to genetic manipulation, as a model for developing new energy and food crops.</p></div></section></div></aside>
